# Ganke Lee — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

## Bottom Line, Up Front

Ganke Lee is a Korean-American adolescent civilian, currently enrolled at Brooklyn Visions Academy, whose entire documented existence — from his introduction at roughly age thirteen in 2011 through present-day appearances — has been organized around a single load-bearing relationship: he is the first, longest-standing, and most trusted civilian confidant of Miles Morales/Spider-Man. He is not a hero, not a villain, not a target, not a victim, not a romantic principal, not a power-holder, not a strategist of any operation larger than a study-night alibi. He is something rarer and more structurally important than any of those: he is *the room in which Miles Morales can stop performing*. That, and the maintenance of that room across years of escalating cost, is the through-line of his life.

The subject's deepest identity is not "best friend" (too sentimental), not "guy in the chair" (too instrumental), and not "sidekick" (too hierarchical and untrue to the relational symmetry on the page). The most accurate one-line designation is **load-bearing confidant** — a civilian who has voluntarily, without compensation, recognition, or authorized support, assumed the cognitive, affective, and operational burden of co-knowing a secret identity, and who has metabolized that burden into a personality structure built almost entirely out of regulatory humor, sublimated construction-play, and chronic affiliative availability.

## The Through-Line

Trace the subject's appearances longitudinally and one pattern is uninterrupted: **Ganke shows up, and someone else's life becomes easier.** Pre-bite, he is Miles's dorm-room ballast against homesickness and the social pressure of a selective boarding school. Post-bite, within the span of a single conversation, he triages the most destabilizing event of Miles's adolescence into an actionable, even *exciting* project — costume identification, secrecy planning, emotional reframing from "I am a freak" to "you are a hero." Across the Ultimate run, the Bendis 616 run, the Ahmed run, and the Ziglar run, through writer transitions, universe collapse, age-up rewrites, and the radical expansion of Miles's social and operational world, the subject's function does not deviate: he arrives, he absorbs, he jokes, he supplies (information, alibi, cover, takeout, perspective), and he recedes precisely as much as is needed without performing the recession.

The through-line is not loyalty in the maudlin sense. It is *constancy without conditions of return*. The subject has never been depicted demanding reciprocity, keeping ledger, escalating grievance for time withheld, or punishing Miles for prioritizing girlfriends, hero-peers, family, or city. This is psychologically anomalous for an adolescent peer of an extraordinary person and is the single most important behavioral datum in the file. Most adolescent secondary figures in this position develop, by the second or third year, some combination of envy, possessiveness, withdrawal, or weaponized victimhood. The subject has developed none of these. The absence is durable, cross-writer, and load-bearing for the entire Miles Morales social architecture.

## The Core Wound — and Why It Doesn't Look Like a Wound

There is no traumatic origin event in the record. No dead parent, no abuse history, no displacement, no documented crisis preceding the disclosure event. This is itself diagnostic: the subject is one of very few characters in the relevant canon whose interior life is not organized around a discrete injury. What he does carry, and what functions as the equivalent of a wound in the structural sense, is **the unaccompanied weight of secret knowledge at a developmental stage that is biologically unequipped for it.**

The wound, named precisely: *he has, since approximately age thirteen, been the sole authorized civilian confidant of a child superhero whose exposure would destroy a family, end a life, and unmake a city's most consequential vigilante operation — and there is no one in his life cleared to help him carry that.* His parents do not know. His teachers do not know. His siblings, if extant in continuity, do not know. Romantic partners are read-in only partially and after the fact. No therapist, no clergy, no mentor figure, no adult in his life has ever been positioned to receive the cognitive-affective load. He is, in the strictest sense, *the only adolescent in his social field doing what he is doing*, and he has been doing it without scheduled relief for the entire arc of his adolescence.

The wound is maintained — and made invisible — by three interlocking mechanisms:

1. **Regulatory humor as primary defense.** Every high-affect moment is metabolized through a joke, a genre reference, a meta-aside, or a deflective comic beat. The humor is not avoidance in the immature sense; it is humor in the Vaillant-mature sense, genuinely soothing both speaker and listener. It works. It works so well that neither Miles nor any other character has ever needed to ask whether the subject is, underneath it, alright. The subject has trained the people around him not to ask.

2. **Sublimation through construction-play.** The LEGO motif is not characterization decoration; it is the externalized neurological signature of self-soothing. Discrete pieces, predictable interfaces, patient assembly, a coherent whole at the end. The subject builds when stressed. He is depicted building constantly. The frequency of the depiction across writers is the somatic record of a chronically activated nervous system finding its way back to baseline through repetitive constructive work.

3. **Caretaker-coded self-effacement.** The subject knows everyone else's interior and discloses almost none of his own. His family is barely on panel. His fears surface only as jokes. His ambitions appear in fragments. He has structured his social presence around being the one who asks, not the one who is asked. This is the central maintenance behavior: by keeping the focus on whoever he is with, he never has to find out whether anyone in his life is equipped to receive him.

The wound is therefore not an event but a *condition of solitude inside intimacy*. He is profoundly connected and structurally alone with what he carries. He has made the solitude look like cheer.

## The Single Reliable Failure Mode

There is one behavior the subject has produced repeatedly, across writers, under stress: **information leakage.** Told to hold the secret, he has, on multiple documented occasions, distributed it — to Judge in the Ultimate run, to peers as the 616 social field expanded, in various smaller disclosures coded as "needing backup." The standard reading is sloppiness or immaturity. The accurate reading is that **the leak is the pressure valve**. The load is genuinely unbearable for one adolescent nervous system to carry indefinitely without authorized relief. When no relief is available, the load discharges sideways — into the nearest trusted node — and the discharge is experienced subjectively as helpfulness ("I'm bringing in another resource") rather than as relief-seeking. This is the single most important operational fact about the subject for anyone planning around him: under sufficient sustained pressure, he will tell *someone*. Not maliciously. Not strategically. Regulatorily.

## Operational Summary

- **Threat to self:** negligible. No suicidality, no substance abuse, no self-harm indicators, no reckless behavior. Risk-averse baseline.
- **Threat to others:** none direct. Indirect threat to Miles's operational security is non-trivial and chronic, via the leak mechanism above.
- **Threat from others:** moderate-to-significant and undermanaged. The subject is the most accessible vector to Miles Morales's identity that exists in civilian life. He is unarmed, untrained, soft-bodied, sedentary, predictable in location (BVA dorm), predictable in routine (laptop, snacks, LEGO), and unguarded. That he has not been targeted is a function of villain incompetence and writer mercy, not of any defensive structure he or anyone else has built around him. This is the largest unaddressed risk in the file.
- **Reliability as confidant:** high in baseline conditions; degrades predictably under sustained load via the leak mechanism. Reliability is *behavioral*, not structural — he wants to keep the secret and usually does; he is not built to keep it forever alone.
- **Reliability as operator:** high within his competence envelope (information, comms, alibi, tech support, emotional triage). Zero outside it. Do not place him in any situation requiring physical action; he has no capacity and no training and will know he has none.
- **Coercion profile:** unstudied but inferable. The subject would likely yield information under credible threat to Miles, to Miles's family, or to his own family, not because he is weak but because his entire value system is organized around protecting the people he loves and he has no framework for the calculus of strategic withholding. He has never been trained, never been pressure-tested, and has never had cause to consider these scenarios. This is a gap.
- **Trajectory:** stable in the short term. In the medium term (continued chronic secret-keeping load without authorized adult relief), predict either (a) a containment failure of operational consequence, or (b) the emergence of somatic or anxious presentation as the humor-and-LEGO regulatory stack begins to under-perform the load. The current regime is sustainable for years, not for decades.

## The One-Sentence File

He is the friend who said *yes, and* to the most consequential disclosure of another person's life, has been saying some version of *yes, and* every day since, and has built his entire personality around the maintenance of a room he has never once been invited to sit down inside.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

## Preface: The Evidentiary Problem

Before any reconstruction: Ganke Lee's origin and childhood are, in the published record, *the most under-documented strata of his existence*. Across more than a decade of appearances — *Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* (2011) onward, Bendis's *Spider-Man* (2016), Ahmed's *Miles Morales: Spider-Man* (2018–2022), Ziglar's continuation, and the various crossover and event tie-ins — Ganke's family of origin appears on panel only in glancing, telephonic, or implied form. His parents are referenced, never centered. His pre-Brooklyn-Visions life is reconstructed almost entirely through trace evidence: speech patterns, object relations, social postures, and the negative space of what he conspicuously does *not* talk about.

This itself is dossier data. A subject whose adolescence is exhaustively recorded but whose childhood is a near-blank does not happen by accident. It is either (a) writerly disinterest in the supporting cast's backstory — true in part, but insufficient — or (b) a behavioral phenotype in which the subject *himself* declines to narrate his pre-history to the people around him. The published Ganke talks constantly about everything except where he came from. The pattern is the file.

What follows therefore distinguishes hard canon (cited), soft canon (inferred from on-panel evidence), and analytic reconstruction (extrapolated from behavioral and ethnographic baseline). All three are marked.

---

## 1. Birth & Vital Statistics

**Name.** Ganke Lee. Korean-American. The surname *Lee* is, as explicitly clarified in *Spider-Man* (2016) #2 in a brief exchange with a peer who assumes otherwise, Korean (Korean: 이 / 리, romanized Lee/Yi/Rhee), not Chinese. The clarification is delivered by Ganke himself, mildly, without heat — but the fact that it had to be delivered, and that Bendis chose to put it on the page, indicates that the misattribution has been a *lifelong recurring micro-friction* for the subject. (Soft canon: the ease and brevity of the correction suggests a script he has run many times since childhood.) The given name *Ganke* is unusual — not a standard Korean given name in either Sino-Korean or native-Korean lexicon; it reads as either a family-specific coinage, an Anglicized phonetic rendering, or a nickname that supplanted a birth name. The published record does not resolve this. Within his peer environment it is treated as his name, full stop.

**Date of birth.** Not specified in canon. Reconstructible: at first appearance (*USM* 2011 #1–2) he and Miles are sixth-graders at Brooklyn Visions, placing his birth circa 1999–2000 in original Ultimate sliding-time, modernized forward in subsequent printings.

**Place of birth.** Not specified. Default reconstruction: New York City metropolitan area, almost certainly one of the outer boroughs or near-suburbs, consistent with his easy fluency in NYC subway logic, food geography, and the Brooklyn Visions catchment.

**Citizenship / generation.** Inferable second-generation Korean-American. His English is fully unaccented, idiomatic, and saturated with the specific grammar of online-native US adolescence; his cultural reference set is Anglophone-default. No on-panel use of Korean is recorded; whether he speaks the language at all is unestablished. This itself is informative — see §5.

---

## 2. Family of Origin

**Parents.** Both presumed living and married; no death, divorce, or estrangement is referenced anywhere in the published record. Mr. and Mrs. Lee appear obliquely: phone calls, mentioned visits, the implied authority behind Ganke's school enrollment and tuition. They are never the source of a crisis. They are never the source of a scene. They function in the record as a *stable off-panel infrastructure* — which is, in a genre that routinely weaponizes parents (Peter's dead Uncle Ben, Miles's incarcerated-then-killed father arc, Gwen's slain father), itself a structural choice. Ganke comes from a family that does not break.

**Inferred parental profile.** Boarding-school enrollment at Brooklyn Visions Academy — a private, selective, tuition-bearing institution — establishes the family as at minimum solidly middle-class, more likely upper-middle, and educationally aspirational. The decision to board a child within commuting distance of New York City (Ganke's family is locally accessible enough that he goes home; he is not from across the country) is a specific parental philosophy: the child benefits from immersive academic environment and peer cohort, even at the cost of daily proximity. This is a recognizable second-generation Asian-American pedagogical strategy — investment in optimized educational environment as the primary parental contribution.

**Siblings.** None depicted. No reference to a brother or sister anywhere in the record. Default reconstruction: only child. The behavioral evidence is consistent: Ganke shows the only-child phenotype of comfortable adult-adjacent verbal register, ease in dyadic intimacy without the sibling-trained competition reflex, and a tendency to bond with a single peer rather than navigate sibling-shaped group politics. He does not have the sibling-vocabulary of negotiated parental attention.

**Extended family.** Unmentioned. No grandparents, aunts, uncles, cousins appear or are named. The cultural-default expectation for a Korean-American household — a network of family elders, holiday gatherings, generational obligations — is conspicuously absent from his on-panel life. This may be writerly omission or it may indicate a family that is itself geographically isolated from extended kin (a not-uncommon immigrant pattern: the parents' generation made a discrete migration; the extended network is in Korea).

---

## 3. Home Environment (Reconstructed)

Brooklyn Visions is the residence of record from age eleven onward; the pre-Visions home must be reconstructed.

**Material conditions.** Comfortable. No food insecurity is ever depicted or implied; Ganke's *relationship to food as plenitude* (constant snacking, casual ordering of takeout, the bowl-of-cereal-at-all-hours posture established in *USM* #2 and recurring through every subsequent run) is the relationship of a child who has never had to think about whether food would be there. He consumes without anxiety, shares without hoarding, wastes occasionally without guilt. This is a fed-childhood signature.

**Object density.** The LEGO obsession — load-bearing in the published record from his first appearances; the Millennium Falcon build is iconic by *USM* #5 — is expensive. Large LEGO sets, accumulated over years, represent not just an interest but a household that funded it. This places his childhood in a material register where a single set could cost upward of \$100–\$800 and parents did not refuse them. Either he was given them (indulgent-parent reading) or he earned/saved for them in a household that did not require his money for survival (autonomy-permissive reading). Both readings agree on basic economic security.

**Technology saturation.** By the time we meet him, Ganke is fully fluent in laptops, comms, code-modification, news-feed monitoring, and the general apparatus of late-2010s/2020s online life. Fluency this deep at age eleven implies *unrestricted-or-lightly-restricted childhood access to networked devices* — again, a class marker and a parenting-style marker. His parents either did not enforce strong screen limits or enforced them in ways he successfully negotiated around. Either way, his cognitive style — associative, reference-saturated, genre-literate — is the cognitive style of a child raised partly by the internet, in addition to and not in conflict with attentive parenting.

**Sound and verbal environment.** The hyperverbal register is established early. He is, in every appearance, a *talker*. This kind of facility is not produced by a quiet home; it is produced by a home in which a child was talked *to*, talked *with*, and permitted — likely encouraged — to talk back. The most economical reconstruction: at least one parent was a verbally engaged adult who treated child-speech as worth answering. Only-child households often produce exactly this pattern.

---

## 4. Formative Bonds & Their Absence

**Pre-Miles peer history.** No childhood best friend is ever referenced. No name from before Brooklyn Visions is invoked. The structural primacy of Miles in his social life — Miles is, functionally, his person from the moment of meeting — suggests that the pre-Miles peer landscape was thin. Three possible reconstructions:

1. *Sociable but unattached.* He had friends, casually; none were primary. Brooklyn Visions delivered him his first true dyadic bond at eleven. This is consistent with the only-child phenotype above.
2. *Marginal in his prior school.* The hyperverbal, genre-saturated, hobby-deep kid is a recognizable type, and that type is not always centered in elementary school peer groups. A child with Ganke's interests may have been *liked but not chosen* in his pre-Visions environment.
3. *Both.* Most likely.

The behavioral evidence on this point is the speed and totality with which he attaches to Miles. The first-night-in-the-dorm bond depicted in *USM* (2011) #1 is not the bond of a child accustomed to forming primary friendships; it is the bond of a child to whom this kind of intimacy is, perhaps, newly available, and who pours himself into it accordingly. Within weeks (*USM* #2–4) Miles is telling him about the spider bite — the most consequential disclosure of Miles's young life — and Ganke is treating it as *their* problem to solve. The collapse of distance is total. This is either a child with prior secure-attachment templates (likely, given the parental reconstruction) *plus* a newly opened channel for peer intimacy that had been previously underutilized.

**Parental bond profile.** Ganke's affect toward authority figures (parents, teachers, administrators across the runs) is *respectful, non-rebellious, mildly evasive when necessary, never hostile*. He is not the child of harsh or punitive parents — children of punitive parents either cower or rebel; Ganke does neither, and his lying about Miles's whereabouts to his own parents (when Miles is, e.g., in another country mid-mission and Ganke is fielding incoming inquiries) is delivered without the somatic tells of fear-conditioned mendacity. He lies smoothly because he has not been catastrophized for lying. He lies *infrequently to his own parents* because he has not had reason to.

This is, in the immigrant-second-generation register, a meaningful profile: parents who instilled discipline through expectation and modeled relationship rather than through punishment. The cultural caricature of the "Asian tiger parent" does not fit him. What fits is *high-expectation, warm-instrumental parenting* — the parents who paid for LEGO and laptops, who placed him in a selective school, who took for granted that he would do his work, and who did not need to police him because the framework was internalized.

**The single shadowed corner.** There is one place the record will not look: Ganke's *interior childhood emotional life*. He has, across more than a decade of publication, never on-panel described his own childhood, his own parents in any depth, his own pre-Miles self. The defense pattern noted in §3 of the workup (*avoidance of self-disclosure*) does not begin at adolescence; it is too smooth, too practiced. The most parsimonious reading is that the *not-talking-about-himself* was established in childhood — likely because the verbal environment of his home, while warm, did not specifically train him in emotional self-narration. Many warm, high-functioning households do not. The child becomes fluent in observation, in synthesis, in commentary, in humor — and not in saying *I felt afraid, I felt small, I felt left out*. Ganke's adult-coded humor-defense system is the lifelong substitute for an emotional-disclosure vocabulary he was never specifically taught.

---

## 5. Cultural-Identity Substrate

**Korean-American identity register.** Ganke's relationship to his Korean heritage is, in the published record, *acknowledged but not foregrounded*. The *Spider-Man* (2016) #2 surname-clarification is essentially the entire on-panel engagement. No food, no holidays, no language, no church, no community organizations, no grandparents, no trips to Seoul — none of the standard ethnographic markers are depicted.

This is again either writerly under-imagination or a real behavioral pattern. If real, it places Ganke in the recognizable second-generation cohort whose ethnic identity is *known, owned when challenged, but not performed* — the kid who corrects the Chinese-assumption with a one-line shrug and does not make it a project, because the project of his life is being himself in the broader American adolescent space he already inhabits fluently. There is no shame here, and no rejection of heritage; there is also no centering of it. This is a coherent identity posture, common, and consistent with parents who themselves may have prioritized assimilation-with-pride over heritage-immersion.

**The micro-friction.** That said: a child who has been called Chinese all his life by classmates, teachers, and strangers — and corrected the error since elementary school — has accumulated a low-grade lifelong friction with being *misread*. This may quietly underwrite an adult-behavioral disposition we see clearly in the record: he is *meticulous about reading other people accurately*. He does not misidentify Miles's moods; he tracks Lana's signals; he picks up on the unspoken among the Spider-cohort. A child whose own identity has been routinely misperceived tends to develop, by compensation, hypervigilant perceptual care toward others'. The corrective stance becomes a perceptual ethic.

---

## 6. Earliest Behavioral Patterns (Pre-Visions, Reconstructed from Continuity)

The following patterns are visible *from the moment of first appearance* and therefore must predate Brooklyn Visions. They are the childhood-formed substrate:

- **Object-mediated self-regulation.** The LEGO behavior is too established at eleven (*USM* #1) to be new. He has been building, alone, for years. This is the load-bearing childhood self-soothing protocol — the discrete-pieces-into-coherent-whole ritual that the workup names as the operational code rendered as object. He learned, early, to manage internal state through patient external assembly. The household permitted, funded, and respected this.

- **Hyperverbal humor as social currency.** A child does not arrive at age eleven already fluent in deflective comedy unless the practice began at five or six. Reconstruction: in his pre-Visions peer environment and likely in his family, *being funny was a reliable way to be received*. He cultivated the skill because it worked.

- **Genre literacy as cognitive scaffolding.** Comics, films, video games, the entire pop-cultural apparatus he applies to lived situations — this is years of consumption. He has been a *reader of stories* since he could read. This is the substrate that makes him able to triage the spider-bite reveal in one conversation: he had templates ready.

- **The caretaker disposition.** From his earliest interactions with Miles, Ganke is *oriented toward Miles's needs* — what does Miles need to hear, what does Miles need to do, what does Miles need to wear (he picks the costume, *USM* #4). This is not learned in a week. The most likely childhood origin: he was, in his family, the child who tracked the adults' moods — perhaps the only-child fluency of being the sole junior in a household, perceptually entrained on the two seniors. He learned to attend to others' interiors early. The cost was the de-prioritization of his own.

- **Resilience baseline.** No depicted childhood trauma. No referenced loss, violence, instability, or rupture. The absence is durable — across multiple writers, no one has retconned a wound. The most honest read is: Ganke had an uneventful childhood by superhero-genre standards, in a stable home, in a culturally fluent second-generation context, with sufficient resources and attentive-enough parents. This is the foundation that makes his adult psychological coherence possible. He arrives at Brooklyn Visions *intact*.

---

## 7. The Pre-Spider Threshold

The final frame of this chronology is the moment immediately before everything. *USM* (2011) #1 opens on a Ganke who is already fully himself: bunked in the BVA dorm, LEGO in progress, laptop open, mouth running, meeting Miles Morales for the first time. The eleven years that produced this child are nearly all off-camera. What walked into that dorm room was a sturdy, fed, talked-to, technology-fluent, object-soothing, story-saturated, only-child, second-generation, non-traumatized, slightly under-disclosed kid with no idea that the boy across the room would, within weeks, become the central organizing fact of his life.

The dossier resumes at the moment of the bite. Everything before is the ground he stood on when it happened — and the reason he did not fall.


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

## Subject: Ganke Lee
## Period Covered: Approx. age 12 through late adolescence — Brooklyn Visions Academy enrollment through full integration as civilian-tier operational support to the Spider-Man (Morales) identity.

---

## 2.1 Pre-BVA Substrate (Pre-Enrollment, Inferred from Behavioral Residue)

The published record opens with Ganke already at Brooklyn Visions Academy — a selective boarding-charter for academically gifted middle-and-upper-school students in downtown Brooklyn. The pre-enrollment period is undocumented in canon, but the residue of it is everywhere in subject's adult-coded behavior and must be reconstructed from that residue.

**Household.** Subject is the child of Korean-American parents (clarified in *Spider-Man* (2016) #2, in dialogue where Ganke explicitly corrects the assumption that "Lee" indicates Chinese descent). The household is functional, middle-class, and minimally depicted; subject is never shown in serious familial conflict, never shown fleeing home, never shown using BVA as an escape. The inference is a household stable enough to be *boring* on panel — the precondition that secure attachment in adolescence almost always requires. Boarding enrollment, in this profile, is not flight; it is an opportunity ratified by parents who could afford the trade of proximity for academic acceleration.

**Schooling pre-BVA.** Subject's cognitive style — associative, reference-saturated, genre-literate, fluent across systems — is not a thing one acquires at thirteen. It is built across years of permissive screen and reading time, unmonitored internet access, and a household that did not pathologize hobbyism. The LEGO obsession predates the published record; subject is depicted as already a *connoisseur* of sets at first appearance, with preferences, completed builds visible in the dorm, and the muscle-memory of an experienced builder. This is years of practice. The likely pre-history is an only-child or eldest-child kid with a lot of unstructured indoor time, a generous Christmas/birthday cycle, and parents who tolerated — perhaps quietly encouraged — the room-full-of-bricks identity.

**Social pre-history.** Subject's interpersonal fluency at BVA — the ease with which he reads sarcasm, the speed at which he attaches to Miles, the absence of social-anxiety markers — suggests a peer-functional childhood, probably one in which he was *liked* but not the social center. The hyperverbal-helpful-funny mode reads as a successfully iterated strategy, not a recent invention. He learned somewhere, before us, that being the kid who *knows things and is fun to be around* was a viable identity slot.

---

## 2.2 BVA Enrollment and the Pre-Bite Roommate Period

Subject and Miles Morales are assigned as dorm roommates at Brooklyn Visions Academy. The earliest depicted interactions (*Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* (2011) #1–2) show a friendship already in motion — not first-day-of-school awkwardness, but the relaxed shorthand of kids who have spent at least several weeks orienting to each other.

The pre-bite baseline is short on the page but diagnostically rich. Subject is established as: the one in the dorm room first; the one with his stuff already organized on his side; the one whose laptop is already open; the one who narrates BVA's social ecology to Miles like a tour guide. He has read the place faster than Miles has. This is the first instantiation of a pattern: **subject orients to environments by mapping them, and offers the map freely to people he likes.** It is a generosity reflex — information is currency, and he gives it away.

The roommate pairing is, in retrospect, the central foundational event of subject's adolescence. Not because he chose it (it was administrative), but because the bond that develops in the first weeks is the bond around which his entire subsequent identity organizes. Pre-bite, the friendship is unremarkable adolescent-male intimacy: shared snacks, after-lights-out conversation, complaint about teachers, mutual tolerance of each other's preferred media. The relationship is *real* before it is *consequential.* This matters psychologically. When the spider-bite event arrives, subject does not bond to Miles *because* Miles is special. The bond exists; the specialness lands on top of it. Subject therefore never relates to Miles primarily as a hero, only ever as a roommate who turned out to also be a hero — a structural fact that protects the friendship across years of escalating asymmetry.

---

## 2.3 The Disclosure Event (Foundational Trauma, Re-coded as Adventure)

*Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* (2011) #2. The defining moment of subject's adolescence and, arguably, of his life.

Miles returns to the dorm room post-bite, terrified, and accidentally activates camouflage in front of subject. The behavioral record of subject's response in this scene is the single most important data point in the entire file.

He does not:
- Scream.
- Recoil physically.
- Tell an adult.
- Question whether to be involved.
- Ask Miles to leave.
- Treat the disclosure as a problem to be managed by distancing.

He does:
- Lean *in*.
- Reach for explanation by analogy ("it's like — ").
- Begin diagnostic curiosity ("what else can you do?").
- Express *delight*.
- Take, within minutes, narrative authorship of what should happen next.

This is not normal adolescent behavior in the face of a friend exhibiting impossible biology. The normative response — even from a genre-literate kid — would include a regulation lag: a beat of fear, a moment of "wait, are you okay," some indication that the body is in survival mode before the mind catches up. Subject shows no regulation lag. His parasympathetic baseline does not appear to spike. He goes from confusion to engagement without passing through threat-response.

Two readings, both true:
1. **Constitutional low threat-reactivity.** Subject's nervous system is unusually calm. This is consistent with everything subsequent — the absence of panic in later crises, the focus-under-pressure, the stable affect baseline. It is a temperamental gift.
2. **Genre as cognitive scaffold.** Subject has spent years pre-loading the templates by which weirdness gets metabolized. A friend with powers does not register as ontologically violating because subject's worldview already contains the schema "friend with powers" as a narratively familiar object. The schema arrives first; the affect is allocated to *which kind of story this is*, not to *whether reality is intact.*

Together, these constitute subject's foundational adaptation: **he is the kid for whom the impossible is procedurally handleable.** This is the trait that makes him irreplaceable to Miles, and it is forged — or, more precisely, *first revealed* — in this scene.

The disclosure event is, by any standard external metric, a trauma: subject becomes, in one conversation, the keeper of a secret whose exposure can destroy a family, end a life, and end his own normal future. The fact that he experiences it as an *adventure* is the central characterological move of his adolescence. The trauma is real; the metabolization is total. The cost — present but unacknowledged in the moment — accrues elsewhere, slowly, in the disclosure-leak pattern that will surface later.

---

## 2.4 The Costume Choice (Authorship Becomes Identity)

*USM* (2011) #4. Subject identifies the black Halloween Spider-Man costume — sitting in the school store as cheap, faintly tasteless, post-Peter-Parker merchandise — as Miles's operational suit.

The choice is presented in canon as a joke that becomes serious, but its formative weight is enormous. In a single decision, subject:
- Established the *visual identity* by which Miles will be known across publication history.
- Took authorship of Miles's hero-presentation at exactly the moment Miles was too disoriented to author it himself.
- Demonstrated a creative-directorial instinct — not just *we should do this*, but *this is the right aesthetic, and here is why.*

This is the second behavioral signature laid down in this period: **subject's contribution to Miles's hero-identity is not auxiliary; it is constitutive.** He does not merely support an existing thing; he co-designs the thing. He is, from the founding moment, a *partner in the construction of the Spider-Man (Morales) persona,* not a witness to it.

Psychologically, this is the moment subject's niche becomes irreplaceable to himself. He does not need to *be* the hero because he has *built* the hero. The LEGO operational code is enacted at human scale: discrete pieces, patient assembly, the satisfaction of a coherent whole. Miles is, in some real sense, his greatest build.

This insight is held privately. Subject never frames it this way out loud. The absence of articulation — and the absence of grandiosity around it — is itself diagnostic of his self-concept's health.

---

## 2.5 The Judge Disclosure (The First Fault Line)

*USM* (2011) #6–10. Subject tells Judge — another BVA friend — that Miles is Spider-Man, against Miles's explicit instruction.

The behavioral details matter:
- The disclosure is not malicious. Subject does not gossip; he frames the share as *operationally necessary* (Miles needs backup; Judge can be trusted; this is for Miles's good).
- The disclosure is not impulsive in the moment; it is the result of accumulated weight. Subject has been carrying the secret alone for weeks. He has no one to talk to about the largest fact in his life.
- The disclosure is rationalized by genre. Subject is thinking in templates — *hero needs a team, hero needs allies, this is how it works* — and the template overrides the explicit instruction from the actual person.
- When confronted by Miles, subject's affective response is *genuine remorse,* not defensive justification. He understood the rule. He broke it anyway. He knows he broke it. He repairs.

This is the foundational instantiation of the **information-dispersal-as-anxiety-discharge** pattern that recurs across subject's publication history. Critically, it is not a betrayal pattern; it is a regulation pattern that *expresses as* boundary breach. The distinction is psychologically real and behaviorally significant: subject does not leak because he wants to harm or because he wants attention; he leaks because *the secret is too heavy and he has no authorized confidant of his own.*

The Judge episode is the first time this pattern shows in the record. It will not be the last. Bombshell, eventually peers across *Spider-Man* (2016) and beyond — each represents an iteration of the same underlying mechanic. Subject's adolescence is shaped, in part, by the fact that he learns the lesson of the Judge incident *interpersonally* (be more careful with Miles's trust) without learning it *structurally* (find a way to discharge the load that does not involve disclosure). The structural problem is never solved, and the leakage therefore recurs.

The fallout with Miles is rapid-repair. Subject apologizes; Miles forgives; the friendship absorbs the rupture without lasting deformation. This is the first demonstration of the *secure-base resilience* of the dyad: ruptures occur, repair is fast, neither party catastrophizes. The pattern of repair will hold across years.

---

## 2.6 The Pre-Disclosure-to-Parents Period (Subject as Infrastructure)

Through *USM* (2011) #10–28, subject's role solidifies. Miles's parents do not know. Miles is patrolling. Subject is in the dorm room. The operational arrangement that defines subject's adolescence crystallizes here:

- **Alibi fabrication.** Subject covers for Miles's absences with parents, RAs, teachers. He develops a working repertoire of plausible excuses, learns which to deploy with which adult, and becomes the front-of-house for Miles's increasingly erratic schedule.
- **Comms and intelligence.** Subject monitors news, police-scanner content, and social-media feeds for incidents that might involve Miles. He learns to identify Spider-Man (Morales) sightings, distinguish them from prior-Parker iconography, and triage what Miles needs to know.
- **Emotional first-response.** Subject is the first person Miles returns to after every patrol. He has, by the end of this period, witnessed Miles in more post-combat states than any other human being. He is, functionally, Miles's decompression chamber.
- **Tech-light support.** Subject is not a hardware genius in the early years — he is a competent hobbyist, not Tony Stark — but he handles costume repair, laptop-based research, and the basic operational hygiene of having a secret identity in a wifi-saturated world. His tech competence grows across years; the early years are more *resourceful generalist* than specialist.

This period forges the operational identity that will define subject across all subsequent runs. He becomes, in his own private self-concept, *the guy in the chair* — though the term will not be retroactively applied to him until later, after MCU influence percolates back into the comics (*Spider-Man* (2016) era). The role is built before it is named.

---

## 2.7 The Death of Ultimate Peter Parker (Indirect Forging Event)

*Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* (2011) #1 occurs after *Ultimate Spider-Man* #160, in which Peter Parker dies. Subject is not present for Peter's death and has no relationship to Peter, but the *world* into which subject enters his hero-adjacent identity is one shaped by that death.

The relevance is psychological. Subject is genre-literate enough to know what happened to the previous Spider-Man. He is not deluded about the stakes. When he encourages Miles to take up the identity — and he does encourage, in the costume-choice scene and in the broader posture of *yes you should do this* — he is doing so with full knowledge that the prior holder of the title died at sixteen.

This is the most morally complex moment of subject's adolescence, and the published record does not interrogate it. It should be marked here: subject's *yes, and* in the foundational period is offered into a context where the cost of *yes* is potentially Miles's life. Subject's apparent insouciance about this should not be read as failure to understand. It should be read as a deliberate (perhaps semi-conscious) decision that Miles having this identity, and surviving it with subject's help, is better than Miles not having it.

This is the closest thing to a covert grandiosity in subject's profile. He does not believe he can save Miles; he believes the *system* of him-plus-Miles is robust enough to keep Miles alive where Peter, alone, was not. The belief is unverifiable, but it is structurally present in every operational choice he makes in this period.

---

## 2.8 The Parental Disclosure (Miles's, Not Subject's)

When Miles eventually discloses to his father (and the broader fallout cascade across *USM* and into the 2016 Bendis run), subject's role shifts. He is no longer the *sole* civilian confidant. The secret-keeping load distributes. The fault-line vulnerability identified in Section 4 of the workup — *no authorized confidant of his own* — eases marginally, because now there is at least a small set of adults who know, and subject is no longer single-pointing the entire civilian information architecture.

The behavioral effect is subtle but real: the information-leakage pattern *reduces in frequency* in proportion to the broadening of the legitimate-knower set. This supports the read that the leakage was load-driven, not character-driven. Subject is more continent with the secret when the secret is less crushing.

The disclosure period also forces subject's first sustained interaction with Miles's parents in a context where they know what he has been doing. Subject is, by all behavioral evidence, handled gently by Rio and Jefferson once they realize the scope of what he has been carrying for their son. There is no scene in canon where he is held accountable for the years of alibi-running; there is an implicit, gracious adult acknowledgment that he kept Miles's life navigable when no adult could. This is, for subject, probably the most emotionally consequential ratification of his adolescent identity he receives from the adult world — and again, in character, he absorbs it without comment.

---

## 2.9 Incursion / Universal Transition (*Secret Wars* → 2016)

The collapse of the Ultimate universe and subject's integration into the prime 616 continuity is, narratively, a hard reset; psychologically, it is *not.* Subject emerges on the other side of *Secret Wars* with all relationships, behavioral patterns, and self-concept intact. The metaphysical event has no internal correlate for him.

This is itself a data point. Subject is constitutionally untroubled by ontological discontinuity — the same trait that allowed him to metabolize the spider-bite reveal in one conversation now allows him to metabolize a continuity reboot without observable affect. He does not grieve the prior universe. He does not seem to remember it as *prior.* He picks up where he is.

The 2016 transition is also the period in which the *guy in the chair* role becomes textually explicit. Subject's operational identity, forged in the Ultimate dorm room across years, is now culturally legible. He is recognized — by readers, by writers, by Miles — as occupying a definite role in the architecture of a hero's life. The role precedes the name; the name, when it arrives, fits.

---

## 2.10 The Bombshell Pursuit (First Identity-Independent Stake)

*Spider-Man* (2016). Subject develops a crush on Lana Baumgartner / Bombshell.

This is the first depicted instance of subject pursuing something *for himself, separable from Miles.* The significance is enormous and easily missed: across the entire prior record, subject's emotional life has been organized around Miles's. The Bombshell pursuit is the first stake subject takes in his own romantic and identity-future as a distinct adolescent person.

The pursuit is also diagnostic. Subject chooses someone:
- Older-coded in affect.
- Criminally adjacent (former villain).
- Socially "off-limits" by BVA-conformist standards.
- Not a peer in his immediate friend group.

This is not a safe choice. The choice reveals a *counter-conformist streak* hidden under subject's rule-following, helpful-friend exterior. Subject is not, in fact, the well-behaved kid he often presents as. He is the well-behaved kid because being well-behaved is operationally useful for his role; given a domain where his role does not constrain him (his own romantic life), he reaches for something interesting, slightly dangerous, narratively rich. The same genre-literacy that makes him a good *guy in the chair* makes him a kid who finds a former-villain crush *appealing* rather than alarming.

The pursuit fumbles, as adolescent crushes do, but its psychological function is achieved: subject has now demonstrated, to himself and the record, that he has a life-axis that is not Miles. This is protective. It is the developmental move that prevents the friendship from becoming the entirety of his identity, and that ensures — as the Ahmed run will later confirm — that subject can recede gracefully when Miles's world expands, because subject has his own world to recede *into.*

---

## 2.11 Summary of Formation

By the end of the period covered here, subject has done the following developmental work:

1. **Metabolized the impossible** without sustained threat response, establishing the foundational adaptation that makes him operationally viable as a hero's civilian partner.
2. **Co-authored a hero identity** through the costume choice and the broader yes-and-engineering of Miles's early career, embedding himself constitutively rather than auxiliarily.
3. **Surfaced and learned (interpersonally if not structurally) the information-dispersal pattern,** through the Judge incident and its repair.
4. **Built an operational repertoire** — alibi, comms, emotional first-response, tech-light support — that constitutes a working professional skillset acquired in adolescence with no curriculum.
5. **Witnessed but did not resolve the structural problem of his own un-confided load,** which remains latent and will recur as the fault line throughout subsequent chronology.
6. **Established a romantic/identity stake outside the Miles-bond,** through the Bombshell pursuit, protecting against role-collapse.
7. **Demonstrated continuity-stability** through the Ultimate-to-616 transition, confirming that his core operating mode is robust across narrative discontinuity.

What is *not* yet done — and what the subsequent chronology will have to address — is any sustained interrogation of the cost. Subject emerges from his formation period as an unusually intact young adult, but the intactness is partly the product of a defense system (humor, sublimation, affiliation, focus-on-other) that has not yet been tested by an event large enough to require him to grieve, fail, or be the one in danger. The formation period builds the operator; the question of whether the *person inside the operator* has been developed at the same rate is open.

The joke has not yet stopped working. It has not yet been asked to.


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*Covering, in publication terms, the long arc from the close of the Ultimate-era roommate establishment (post-*Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* (2011) #15 onward) through *Cataclysm* (2013–14), the *Miles Morales: Ultimate Spider-Man* (2014) solo run, the universe-collapse of *Secret Wars* (2015), the integration year of *Spider-Man* (2016) #1–21, the *Champions* (2016) cross-traffic, the brief but consequential *Spider-Men II* (2017) shadow, the *Spider-Geddon* and *Spider-Verse*–adjacent intrusions, and the early Ahmed-run consolidation through *Miles Morales: Spider-Man* (2018) #1–25. This is the period in which Ganke Lee stops being a comic-relief roommate and becomes — structurally, operationally, behaviorally — the load-bearing civilian infrastructure of another person's superhero life.*

---

## I. The Consolidation Phase (post-*USM* #15 → *Cataclysm*)

The earliest signature of the middle period is the **transition from reactive confidant to proactive operator.** In the opening Ultimate arcs, Ganke is the recipient of information — Miles comes home, Miles confesses, Miles displays, Ganke marvels. By the time of the late Bendis Ultimate issues, the directionality has reversed in observable proportion. The subject is the one initiating contact: paging Miles between classes, intercepting him in stairwells with phone-screens held up ("you need to see this"), opening conversations with already-loaded news feeds. The behavioral marker is small but durable: across roughly three dozen panels in this window, Ganke is depicted *with a device already on* before Miles enters frame. He has been working on the problem before the problem has arrived.

This is the consolidation of his **guy-in-the-chair niche**, but it predates the *Homecoming*-era pop-cultural codification of that role by years. He invents his own job. No one asks him to monitor police scanners; he begins to. No one tasks him with fabricating cover stories for Miles's absences from Brooklyn Visions; he begins to. The early Ultimate-era alibis are crude (illness, library, "he's in the bathroom") and rapidly become more sophisticated — by *Cataclysm* he is producing dated, plausible, layered cover that accounts for who-saw-what, including pre-empting Rio and Jefferson Morales's calls to the dorm phone with prepared scripts.

**Cataclysm (2013–14)** is the first arc in which the load on the subject becomes properly visible. Galactus is in the sky over the Ultimate New York. Miles is gone for days. Ganke is left in the dorm room, alone, with knowledge no other civilian his age possesses, watching his best friend possibly die on a live feed. The behavioral record across this arc is consistent: he does not call Rio. He does not break protocol. He sits with his laptop, refreshes feeds, and waits. This is the earliest evidence of what becomes a defining capacity — **stillness under existential dread.** The body-at-rest, mind-engaged posture acquires a heavier meaning here. He is not lounging; he is enduring.

The aftermath of *Cataclysm*, in which Miles is presumed dead and the Ultimates believe him gone, is one of the few windows in the full record in which the subject is observed *without Miles to organize him.* The Ultimate-era issues handle this only glancingly, but the texture is there: he is depicted alone in the dorm, fewer snacks visible, the LEGO output increases. He builds. He waits. When Miles returns, Ganke is not depicted weeping; he is depicted, in a now-iconic beat, mid-build, looking up, registering, returning to the build, *then* speaking. The deflection is constitutive. Affect arrives, is metabolized, is contained, and only then is language permitted.

---

## II. The Solo-Run Recalibration (*Miles Morales: Ultimate Spider-Man*, 2014)

The 2014 solo Ultimate run repositions the subject. Miles's mother is dead. Miles has been gone a year-plus. Jefferson and Miles are estranged. Into this newly fractured family-of-origin field, Ganke functions as **the surrogate continuity** — the one relationship that has not broken, the one room (the dorm) that is still the room it was.

Three behavioral patterns crystallize in this run:

**(1) The protocol of return.** Whenever Miles arrives back at the dorm — bloodied, exhausted, dissociated, mid-crisis — Ganke executes a near-identical sequence: notice without alarm, offer of food (almost always something already opened or in arm's reach), question phrased as joke ("you look like garbage, professionally"), no demand for narrative, the laptop closed only if Miles sits down. The subject has effectively built a **decompression airlock** for his friend, a low-pressure room where the hero can return to civilian metabolism without being interrogated. It is the most psychologically sophisticated thing he does, and he does it without naming it.

**(2) The expanding cover-story corpus.** By mid-2014 the alibis are no longer per-event; they form a coherent **fictional Miles** maintained in parallel — a continuous narrative of an ordinary teenager that the subject keeps current in his head and deploys as needed to parents, RAs, teachers, classmates. This is enormous cognitive load. It is also the most underweighted line item in any assessment of him: he is, functionally, the maintainer of a *second person*, a phantom Miles Morales who exists only in the explanations Ganke gives other people about where the real one is. Identity-management work of this scale is what intelligence officers do for assets. He is doing it for free, for love, at fifteen.

**(3) Selective non-disclosure to Miles himself.** This is the most easily missed development. In the early Ultimate issues, Ganke tells Miles everything he knows. From the solo run onward, the subject begins to *filter upward*: tracking news Miles doesn't see, withholding items that would distract him mid-crisis, surfacing them later. He is editing the world Miles perceives. There is no evidence this is ever discussed. It is a unilateral curatorial decision the subject makes about his friend's information environment. The implication is significant — by mid-2014 he has decided he has standing to manage what his friend knows.

---

## III. The Universe-Collapse and Integration (*Secret Wars* → *Spider-Man* 2016)

The most consequential structural event of the subject's life occurs almost entirely off-page from his perspective: the Ultimate universe ends. He is folded into the 616. The continuity-integration is handled, narratively, by *Spider-Man* (2016) #1, in which Ganke is simply present, as if he had always been there, with composite memories of a life that now includes both Ultimate and 616 elements.

What is striking, behaviorally, is the **absence of reaction.** The subject does not, on panel, register the metaphysical fact of his own reconstitution. He proceeds. Two readings: (a) the writers elected not to interrogate it; (b) the character, as constituted, *would* not interrogate it — he is template-driven, pattern-matching, and would assimilate the new circumstance as the new circumstance and route around the ontological question. The latter is more interesting and, given the consistency of his cognitive style across other novel scenarios, probably truer. The subject has a high tolerance for premise. He plays the game in front of him.

The 616 dorm room at Brooklyn Visions becomes, across *Spider-Man* (2016) #1–21, **the most documented physical space in the full record.** Bunk bed (Ganke on bottom, consistent across nearly every issue), Miles's posters, Ganke's LEGO display surface (which grows across the run — a Death Star is depicted in progress over multiple issues, completed somewhere around #10–12, then partially disassembled to make room for a new build), two laptops, a small fridge or snack drawer in arm's reach of the bottom bunk, a desk chair almost never used as a desk chair (used as a foot-rest, a coat-hook, a laundry stack). The geography is stable enough that a reader could draw it from memory. This stability is not accidental. The subject **needs the room to be the room.** Disruption of the dorm's spatial arrangement is one of the few things that visibly destabilizes him on panel.

---

## IV. The Bombshell Subplot — The First Outward Pursuit

Across roughly *Spider-Man* (2016) #6 through the late teens of the run, Ganke pursues Lana Baumgartner. The pattern, in granular form:

- **Initial encounter.** Bombshell crosses into Miles's circle (her mother's incarceration arc and her recruitment-adjacent involvement with the Avengers periphery). Ganke notices her, immediately. The reaction is uncharacteristic for him — he is, briefly, **non-verbal.** The hyperverbal subject loses speech around her in the first one or two interactions, a defense-stripping that does not recur with any other person in the record.
- **Approach behavior.** Once language returns, it returns over-corrected: he over-talks, references everything he knows about her (which she finds, in turn, partly flattering and partly unsettling — he has done research), and offers help she did not request. The behavior is anxious-eager, not strategic, exactly as the prior assessment notes.
- **The crucial structural fact.** He pursues someone whose history (juvenile criminal record, villain-adjacent past) is socially disqualifying by the standards of a rule-following honors student. He does not appear to register the contradiction. This is the **counter-conformist streak under the rule-follower exterior**, and it is the single best evidence in the full record that the subject's compliance is *situational and chosen*, not temperamental. Given a stake that matters to him, he picks the unapproved option without hesitation.
- **Failure modes.** The relationship does not consolidate into stable couplehood. The fumbling is real and the dropoff is gradual rather than dramatic. Notably, Ganke does not appear to grieve it heavily. The romantic vulnerability is genuine; the recovery is quick. This is consistent with the broader affective profile — broad but shallow range, fast metabolism of disappointment, no protracted depressive aftermath.

The Bombshell arc is the closest the subject comes, in the middle period, to having **a story of his own.** The fact that it is a B-plot inside Miles's book is structurally telling. He is granted interiority on the page only when the interiority can be folded into the larger Miles-narrative.

---

## V. The Disclosure Cascade and the Identity-Reveal Crisis

The single most consequential behavioral event of the middle period is the **expansion of the circle of people who know.** The pattern, recapped longitudinally:

- **Phase 1 (Ultimate, pre-middle).** Ganke tells Judge. Confronted. Apologizes. The principle of secrecy is restated.
- **Phase 2 (Ultimate solo run).** Information leaks more diffusely — Ganke is the indirect vector for Bombshell knowing, for various peers piecing things together.
- **Phase 3 (*Spider-Man* 2016, middle of run).** A widening cluster of Brooklyn Visions students come to know or strongly suspect, with Ganke as the proximate cause in at least some cases — either through direct disclosure under social pressure, through carelessness with materials (the dorm room is a forensic gold mine: the costume is *in the room*), or through the cumulative weight of his cover-story system finally producing inconsistencies a peer notices.

The longitudinal read is unambiguous: **the subject cannot indefinitely hold the secret alone, and the system he has built to hold it leaks structurally, not incidentally.** The leaks are not the result of carelessness in the conventional sense; they are the result of one adolescent maintaining the cognitive load of two people's identities, in a residential environment with no privacy, for years, with no authorized confidant of his own. The wonder is not that the secret diffuses; the wonder is that it diffuses as slowly as it does.

When Miles confronts him about each cascade — and Miles does, repeatedly — Ganke's response pattern is consistent: immediate acknowledgment, no excuse-making, joke as the affect-cap, sincere apology, and **no observable behavioral change going forward.** He says he will do better. He means it. He does not, in fact, do better. The pattern continues. This is one of the few places where the subject's stated self-understanding diverges from his actual operating system; he believes he is capable of containment that he is not, in practice, capable of under sustained load.

---

## VI. The Champions Period and the Outward Pull

When Miles co-founds Champions (with Kamala Khan, Sam Alexander, Amadeus Cho, Viv Vision, and the time-displaced young Cyclops), the subject's life experiences its first major **bandwidth competition.** Miles is on a Quinjet half the time. Miles has new peers who *know*. Miles has a chosen family of equals.

The subject's response is the single most psychologically impressive sustained behavior in the record: **he does not contract.** The predicted secondary-character escalation — possessiveness, sulking, manufactured crises to recapture attention, passive-aggressive sabotage — does not occur. Across the entire Champions cross-traffic window, Ganke is depicted welcoming Miles home from team deployments with the same airlock-protocol he developed in the Ultimate solo run, asking after the team by name (he learns and uses their names; he does not refer to them as "your hero friends"), and integrating them, where possible, into the dorm ecosystem.

There is a small, easily-missed beat in this window in which Kamala Khan visits the dorm. The subject is, briefly, **starstruck in a way he is not around Miles.** He recovers; he is hospitable; he treats her as a person rather than a phenomenon. But the brief loss of composure is data: he can fanboy. He does not fanboy at Miles. The proximity has fully demystified the hero he lives with; the heroism elsewhere retains wonder. This is healthier than the inverse would be, but it is also a quiet loss — somewhere in the middle period, he stopped being able to see his best friend as a marvel.

---

## VII. The Older-Miles Shadow (*Spider-Men II*, 2017)

Brief, but worth marking. *Spider-Men II* introduces the existence of a 616-native, adult Miles Morales — a parallel-life figure whose existence destabilizes the younger Miles's understanding of his own continuity. The subject is at the periphery of this arc, but the secondary effect on him is observable: in the issues bracketing the *Spider-Men II* events, his behavior toward Miles is **subtly more protective**, the cover-story production more meticulous, the airlock-protocol more elaborate. He does not articulate what he is responding to. He simply, briefly, holds tighter. When the arc resolves, the elevated protectiveness dissipates. The subject is responsive to threats to Miles's *identity continuity* in ways he does not consciously narrativize.

---

## VIII. The Ahmed Transition (*Miles Morales: Spider-Man*, 2018 → ~#25)

The 2018 relaunch backgrounds the subject relative to his peak centrality in the 2016 series. The dorm becomes a less frequent setting; Miles's familial home and the wider Brooklyn street-level life take precedence. New figures enter: Barbara Rodriguez, Starling (Tiana Toomes), Shift, eventually Billie. The subject's screen time contracts.

What is notable across this contraction is, again, the **absence of compensatory behavior.** He does not stage interventions to reclaim narrative space. He does not develop a grievance arc. He recedes when Miles's life requires he recede; he returns when called. The longitudinal consistency is, by the end of this window, extraordinary. Across more than a decade of publication, across the collapse of one universe and the integration into another, across romantic disappointments, expanding peer circles, the strain of a multi-year secret, and a structural demotion in the narrative attention his friend's book pays him, the subject has not visibly altered his core operating system.

But the Ahmed run does seed one item worth flagging for later chronology: **the subject's social world begins, for the first time, to be depicted as having components Miles is not the center of.** Brief panels show Ganke with classmates not previously seen, in study contexts, in casual peer settings. These are micro-glimpses, not subplots. But they suggest that the subject has, at some point in the middle period, quietly begun building **a life adjacent to but not solely defined by** the Miles-axis. This is healthy. It is also, given the disclosure-cascade pattern, a new attack surface — every additional peer is a potential leak vector, and the subject's containment capacity has not improved.

---

## IX. Pattern Evolution Across the Middle Period — Summary of Drifts

The granular longitudinal record shows the following measurable drifts from beginning to end of the middle period:

- **Sleep architecture.** Worsens. Late-night laptop scenes increase in frequency. The subject is more often the last figure depicted awake in any given issue.
- **Caloric intake.** Stable to slightly elevated. Snack inventory in the dorm grows. Food remains primary self-regulation.
- **LEGO output.** Increases under stress, decreases under acute Miles-presence. Functions as countercyclical regulator. The Death Star build of *Spider-Man* (2016) is the most documented single build; smaller modular sets cycle through the desk surface continuously.
- **Verbal output.** Stable-high. No measurable decrease under any condition short of Bombshell's direct presence.
- **Disclosure-leak frequency.** Roughly constant per unit of time, despite the subject's stated commitments after each incident. The pattern is robust.
- **Affective range on panel.** Unchanged. Light-positive baseline, joke-deflection of high affect, brief windows of unguarded earnestness (typically after Miles has returned from near-death events, and typically lasting fewer than two pages before the humor reasserts).
- **Cover-story system complexity.** Increases monotonically. By end of middle period, the fictional-Miles he maintains is multi-week internally consistent and contains contingent branches.
- **Self-disclosure to others.** Effectively flat at near-zero. The subject's family is barely depicted. His fears are not articulated except as jokes. His ambitions surface only in fragments — references to coding interests, to college, to game design, never elaborated. He talks constantly and reveals little. This is the most durable feature of him across the entire middle period.

---

## X. Closing Frame

The defining middle period is, in net, the time in which the subject **becomes irreplaceable without becoming visible.** He builds, alone and unprompted, the civilian infrastructure that allows another person to be a superhero — the alibi corpus, the decompression airlock, the curated information environment, the dorm-as-haven, the always-on monitoring posture. He sustains this through universe-collapse, family death, peer expansion, romantic disappointment, and the steady-state existential pressure of holding a secret that could destroy the person he most loves.

He does it without compensation, without recognition, without complaint, and — most diagnostically — **without an authorized confidant of his own.** The asymmetry that opens the period (Miles has a secret; Ganke holds it for him) closes the period unchanged. No one has been recruited to hold Ganke for Ganke. He has not recruited anyone. He has not, on the available record, recognized the need.

The fault line identified in the prior workup — the absence of an authorized confidant, the inevitability of containment breakdown under chronic load — is, by the end of the middle period, **load-tested but not failed.** The leaks are there. The subject is not. Whether that asymmetry survives the next period is the question the record, at this cutoff, leaves open.


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

## Temporal Frame

This section covers the period from approximately the conclusion of Saladin Ahmed's *Miles Morales: Spider-Man* (2018–2022, ending with issue #42) through Cody Ziglar's relaunched *Miles Morales: Spider-Man* (2022–present) and the *Spider-Man: Spider-Boy* / *Gang War* / *Rise of the Power Cosmic* adjacencies, into the current present-day status as of last documented appearance. The compressed in-universe span is roughly one to two academic years; the publication span is roughly three years. Subject is now late-adolescent (junior/senior year coded), no longer the rounded middle-school-affect figure of the *Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* (2011) era, and has been drawn and written progressively into a young-adult register — taller line, slimmer face, dryer vocal cadence, longer thought-units between deflections.

## The Ahmed-to-Ziglar Handoff: Background Period

In the final stretch of Ahmed's run, Subject was demonstrably backgrounded — present, accounted for, but orbital. The narrative gravity had shifted to Miles's expanding hero-adjacent family: Billie (infant sister), Shift, Starling, Barbara Rodriguez, and the displaced-Brooklyn community plotlines. Subject's contributions in this stretch are characterized by short scenes — kitchen-table tech consultations, dorm-room debriefs, the occasional brokered alibi with the Morales parents. He is depicted increasingly *off-panel-as-implied*: Miles refers to "Ganke handled it," "Ganke's on comms," "I told Ganke" without Subject appearing. This is operationally meaningful — Subject has, by his own internal preference (see Personality § Core Drives), settled into the load-bearing infrastructure of Miles's double life to the degree that his presence is *assumed* rather than *staged*. In total-surveillance terms: he has achieved background-process status.

During this stretch, the Bombshell subplot decays without explicit resolution on panel. Lana Baumgartner's continuity is pulled in other directions; Subject's romantic life goes dormant in print. There is no breakup scene; there is a fade. This is a recurring narrative-environmental hazard for him — he is the kind of character whose subplots are dropped, not closed — and he appears to absorb it without depicted protest. The behavioral inference: he registers the loss privately, processes it through increased LEGO output and screen time (both depicted in incidental panels of the late Ahmed run), and does not re-raise it with Miles. Consistent with the avoidance-of-self-disclosure pattern previously catalogued.

## Ziglar Run, Opening Movement (*Miles Morales: Spider-Man* vol. 2, 2022–)

Ziglar reinstalls Subject in the foreground from the first arc. The voice shifts subtly: jokes are slightly dryer, references slightly more current, the cadence slightly more young-adult than adolescent. The LEGO motif is preserved but de-emphasized in favor of laptop-and-coding imagery — Subject is depicted more often at a keyboard, often with multiple monitors in the dorm. Snacks remain constant (energy drink cans, instant noodle cups, the specific staging of his side of the dorm room reading as controlled disorder).

Key developments under Ziglar:

**(a) The "guy in the chair" infrastructure is formalized.** Subject is shown running a multi-screen comms rig from the BVA dorm — police-band scraper, news-feed aggregator, a self-written script for filtering Spider-Man-adjacent chatter from social media. This is a *step-change* from the laptop-and-vibes operational mode of prior runs. Subject has built, over the gap years, what amounts to a small-scale OSINT pipeline. He maintains and patches it himself. He has not, notably, *outsourced* or *recruited* — this is a solo-operator setup, and the operational-security read is favorable: the fewer hands on the tools, the fewer leak vectors. (A development that, paired with the historical disclosure-compulsion, suggests Subject has *learned* the cost of distribution and has at the technical-architecture level engineered against his own pattern. This is significant maturation.)

**(b) The introduction of Shift and the expanded Spider-team complicates Subject's role.** When Miles acquires a "team" — proto-Spider-family figures including Shift — Subject is no longer the *only* confidant. His monopoly on hero-adjacent disclosure inside Miles's civilian life is now diluted at the hero-adjacent layer. Behavioral observation: Subject does *not* exhibit territorial protest. He instead expands his function — he becomes the *interface* between Miles's hero-team and Miles's civilian-school-family life, a translator role. He is the one who knows both the lab-coats-and-codenames register and the call-Miles's-mom register and can move between them. This is a niche-evolution worth marking: Subject did not lose ground when the field expanded; he repositioned to the *seam*, which is structurally harder to displace.

**(c) The Selim arc / corrupted-Miles ("Beyonder-Miles") storyline.** During Ziglar's extended arc concerning the corrupted Miles construct and the family-targeting threat, Subject is depicted under direct stress for the first time in years. He is shown unable to reach Miles for an extended period; the comms rig is depicted as anxiously over-utilized (multiple panels of Subject at the keyboard at 3 AM, energy drinks accumulating). He does not collapse. He does, however, *delegate up* — he contacts Rio Morales and, separately, signals the broader Spider-network. This is a critical behavioral data point: Subject has, after a decade of unilateral secret-holding, developed the operational discipline to *escalate to authorized parties* rather than discharge laterally. The disclosure-compulsion pattern has been redirected from "tell a peer because the load is heavy" to "alert the appropriate adult/network node because the situation requires it." This is the difference between regulation and operations, and Subject has crossed that line.

**(d) Academic trajectory.** Subject is depicted in upper-form coursework at BVA, with implied college-track positioning. References in dialogue (incidental, never centered) place him as taking advanced CS/engineering coursework, possibly dual-enrollment with an external program. He has not been depicted applying anywhere specific on-panel, but the technical sophistication of his comms rig and the increased coding-imagery suggest he is moving toward a real specialization, not merely a hobby trajectory.

## The Gang War / Crossover Period

During the line-wide *Gang War* crossover, Subject's role narrows again as Miles is pulled into Tombstone-faction conflict and adult-hero coordination dominates. Subject is shown providing tactical intel — building threat-maps of contested Brooklyn blocks, scraping criminal radio chatter, providing real-time positioning data — from the dorm. He is not depicted in the field. He is not endangered. The structural-safety pattern catalogued in § Medical holds: writers continue to keep Subject's body unscathed. This is now long enough and consistent enough across writers to read as a *load-bearing canonical convention* — Subject is the unhurt one, deliberately preserved as the stable-base contrast to Miles's accumulating wear.

There is a brief depicted moment during this period — Subject alone in the dorm, comms quiet, Miles unreachable for several hours, Subject staring at a half-built LEGO set without proceeding — that registers as the closest thing on panel to the *latent fault line* identified in § Psychiatric. He does not break. He does not cry. He sits. Then he resumes building. Then Miles calls in. The moment is brief; it is also, in the entire publication record, one of the only depictions of Subject *alone with the cost of his role*. It should be flagged for longitudinal tracking — it is the first crack in the joke-as-load-bearing-structure, and writers may or may not return to it.

## Romantic and Social Status, Current

The Bombshell thread remains officially unresolved; she has not reappeared in Subject's orbit during the Ziglar period. Subject has not been depicted in a new romantic pursuit on panel. Social orbit is depicted as broader than in the Bendis era — he is shown with BVA peers beyond the original Miles/Judge axis, including study-group and gaming-group configurations, but no single named replacement-friendship has emerged. He remains, in effect, *Miles's best friend and a cohort of acquaintances* — the structure has not diversified into a second primary bond. This is consistent with his caretaker-style relational pattern: deep singular attachment, broad shallow secondaries.

Family remains under-depicted, as it has been throughout publication history. The Lee parents have not been substantively staged. Subject has not been shown going home for breaks; school continuity dominates. This is now a publication-record anomaly worth naming — Subject is one of the few major supporting figures in Miles's circle whose family of origin is essentially a *narrative null*. Whether by writers' choice or by Subject's own depicted preference for the BVA environment as primary home, the result is the same: he is institutionally housed, peer-bonded, parent-distal.

## Current State (Present Tense Assessment)

**Location.** Brooklyn Visions Academy, upper-form dormitory, same room as Miles Morales (continuity-preserved across the *Secret Wars* transition and three writer regimes). Comms-and-coding setup operational.

**Role.** Primary civilian confidant and information-infrastructure operator for Spider-Man (Miles Morales). Interface node between hero-team and civilian-family layers of Miles's life. Background-process status in the broader Spider-narrative; foreground when summoned.

**Disposition.** Stable. Voice has matured to dry-young-adult cadence. Defensive structure (humor, sublimation through building/coding, affiliation) is intact and functioning. The disclosure-compulsion has been operationally redirected into authorized-escalation channels — the most significant psychological development of the period. Affect remains light-positive at baseline; the single depicted moment of solitary cost (the LEGO-paused-mid-build panel) is the only countervailing data point and has not been narratively followed.

**Trajectory.** On track for college-level technical specialization; on track for continued infrastructural role in Miles's hero-life; on track, per the established pattern, to be preserved bodily and to absorb the chronic-cortisol load of secret-keeping through his existing regulatory protocols. The latent fault line — what happens when the joke stops working — remains latent. It has not been triggered. It has, once, been *brushed*.

**Risk posture.** Low acute, low-moderate chronic. The single risk vector worth active monitoring is the cumulative load of a now nearly-decade-long secret-keeping role at adolescent-into-young-adult developmental stage, without an authorized confidant of his own. He has built the technical infrastructure to outlast his own anxious patterns.
